<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="module-community-health-unit-epidemiology">MODULE: COMMUNITY HEALTH UNIT: EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#module-community-health-unit-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>By Carey Francis Okinda
June-July 2015</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="lesson-1-introduction-to-epidemiology">Lesson 1: INTRODUCTION TO EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lesson-1-introduction-to-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>At the end of the lesson the learner will be able to: -</p>
<ol>
<li>Define key words</li>
<li>Explain epidemiological concepts and terms</li>
<li>Compare the clinical medicine and epidemiology</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-0-introduction">1.0 INTRODUCTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-0-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>The word derived from 'epidemics' and from Greek words "epi" = on or upon; "demos" = the common people; "logos" = study</li>
<li>Epidemiology is the study of:</li>
<li>i) Something that afflicts the population</li>
<li>ii) Distribution of disease and its determinants in human populations in order to control health problems</li>
<li>iii) Disease occurrence (frequency, distribution, determinants) in human populations and application of that knowledge to control health problems</li>
<li>iv) Distribution and determinants of health related states or events in specified human populations and the application of this study to control of health problems.</li>
<li>Epidemiologists are interested in occurrence of disease with respect to time, place and persons</li>
<li>The key aspects of the definitions include</li>
<li>i) Study</li>
<li>Involves surveillance, observation, hypothesis testing, analytical research and experiments</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Levels of scientific study of disease involve the
a) Sub molecular or molecular level (e.g., cell biology, biochemistry, and immunology);
b) Tissue or organ level (e.g., anatomic pathology)
c) Level of individual patients (e.g., clinical medicine); and
d) Level of populations (e.g., epidemiology).</li>
</ul>
<p>ii) Determinants(PBSCB)</p>
<ul>
<li>Factors or events that are capable of bringing about change in health which include physical, biological, social, cultural and behavioural factors that influence health</li>
</ul>
<p>iii) Distribution</p>
<ul>
<li>This refers to the analysis of disease by person, place and time.</li>
<li>The frequency of disease may vary from one population group to another (Give examples)</li>
</ul>
<p>iv) Population</p>
<ul>
<li>A group of people with a common characteristic, e.g. place of residence, gender, age, or occurrence of event</li>
<li>To measure frequency of disease in population consider:</li>
<li>Number of people affected (cases)</li>
<li>Size of the population from which the cases arise</li>
<li>Amount of time that the population is followed</li>
<li>Two major types of populations
a) Fixed or closed population:</li>
<li>A population in which the same individuals are followed from the start of the study (follow up period) until its end</li>
<li>Membership is defined by a life event and is permanent</li>
<li>Useful for computing cumulative incidence
b) Dynamic or open population:</li>
<li>A population in and out of which individuals move during the follow up period (e.g. the population of a town)</li>
<li>Membership based on a changeable condition and is transitory</li>
<li>Useful for computing incidence rate or density
v) Health phenomena (morbidity and mortality)</li>
<li>Epidemiology investigates many different kinds of health outcomes, from infectious diseases to chronic diseases, and various states of health such as disability, injury, limitation of activity, and mortality</li>
<li>Other health outcomes have included positive functioning of the individual and active life expectancy as well as health-related events including mental illness, suicide, drug addiction and injury</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>vi) Control of the health problem</p>
<ul>
<li>Reduce the risk factors</li>
<li>Reduce or stop transmission of infectious agents</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-0-scope-of-epidemiology">2.0 SCOPE OF EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-0-scope-of-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Epidemiology is concerned with efforts to:</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Describe (enumerate/ count)</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>To describe the health status of a population means to enumerate the cases of disease, to obtain relative frequencies of the disease within subgroups, and to discover important trends in the occurrence of disease.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Explain (etiology)</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>To explain the etiology of disease means to discover causal factors as well as to discover modes of transmission.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="3">
<li>Predict (risks)</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>To predict the occurrence of disease is to estimate the actual number of cases that will develop as well as to identify the distribution within populations. Such information is crucial to planning interventions and allocation of resources.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="4">
<li>Control</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>To control the distribution of ds, the epidemiologic approach is used</li>
<li>to prevent the occurrence of new cases of diseases,</li>
<li>to eradicate existing cases, and</li>
<li>to prolong the lives of those with diseases</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-0-history-of-epidemiology">3.0 HISTORY OF EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-0-history-of-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Started off as the study of various disease epidemics</li>
<li>Concerned with modern epidemics, to include infectious (communicable, molecular), chronic (cancer, cardiovascular), nutritional and non-communicable diseases (injury, mental), clinical (diagnostics, therapy, prognosis) and reproductive health issues</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-0-uses-of-epidemiological-data">4.0 USES OF EPIDEMIOLOGICAL DATA<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-0-uses-of-epidemiological-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Examine causation</li>
<li>Study natural history of the disease</li>
<li>Description of the health status of population (the host)</li>
<li>Determine the relative importance of causes of illness, disability and death</li>
<li>Evaluation of interventions</li>
<li>Identify risk factors</li>
<li>Changes of the pattern of infectious diseases</li>
<li>Discovery of new infections</li>
</ol>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
3
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-0-importance-of-epidemiology">5.0 IMPORTANCE OF EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-0-importance-of-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Describes the geographical distribution and size of disease</li>
<li>Identify causes of disease</li>
<li>Identifies who is at risk of the disease</li>
<li>Determines the risk factors for the disease</li>
<li>Design programs to prevent, control and treat the disease</li>
<li>Monitor the disease to establish its trends over time</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="6-0-basic-concepts-in-epidemiology">6.0 BASIC CONCEPTS IN EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-0-basic-concepts-in-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Description and quantification of disease events in populations</li>
<li>Disease determinants – intrinsic and extrinsic determinants</li>
<li>Analysis of epidemiological data</li>
<li>Presentation of epidemiological data (using descriptive statistics)</li>
<li>Investigation of disease problems</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="7-0-epidemiological-terms">7.0 EPIDEMIOLOGICAL TERMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-0-epidemiological-terms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>Concept</th><th>Description</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>1.</td><td>Aetiology</td><td>Cause(s) of disease</td></tr><tr><td>2.</td><td>Agent or risk factor</td><td>A biological or non-biological substance whose presence or absence is associated with increased probability that a disease will occur</td></tr><tr><td>3.</td><td>Carrier</td><td>An apparently healthy person who harbours an infectious agent for some time period and with the ability to transmit it to those who do not have it</td></tr><tr><td>4.</td><td>Endemic</td><td>Occurrence of a disease at the expected level within a geographical area</td></tr><tr><td>Is a disease that occurs in a population with predictable regularity and with only minor deviations from its expected frequency of occurrence</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Holo endemic - a disease for which a high prevalence of infection begins early in life and affects most or all children, leading to a state of equilibrium, such that the adult population shows evidence of the disease much less frequently than in children</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Hyper endemic - a disease that is constantly present at a high incidence and/or prevalence rate and affects all age groups equally (is an endemic disease that affects a high proportion of the population at risk.)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Hypo endemic - is an endemic disease that affects a small proportion of the population at risk</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Mesoendemic is an endemic disease that affects a moderate proportion of the population at risk</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>5.</th><th>Epidemic</th><th>Disease occurrence at a level that is clearly higher than the expected within a geographical area</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>6.</td><td>Host</td><td>An organism capable of being infected or affected by a disease causing agent</td></tr><tr><td>7.</td><td>Immunity</td><td>Ability to resist disease attack</td></tr><tr><td>8.</td><td>Incidence</td><td>Number of new cases of a disease within a specified period</td></tr><tr><td>9.</td><td>Incubation period</td><td>Interval between exposure to an infectious agent and the disease it causes</td></tr><tr><td>The period between infection and clinical onset of the disease</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Clinical incubation period –</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Biological incubation period -</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>10.</td><td>Isolation</td><td>Voluntary or compulsory separation and confinement of those known or suspected to be infected with a contagious disease agent (whether ill or not) to prevent further infections (transmission-based precautions are imposed.)</td></tr><tr><td>11.</td><td>Latent period</td><td>The time from infection to infectiousness</td></tr><tr><td>12.</td><td>Life expectancy</td><td>The average period that a person may expect to live</td></tr><tr><td>13.</td><td>Pandemic</td><td>Infectious disease that has spread through human populations across a large region (multiple continents) or even worldwide.</td></tr><tr><td>14.</td><td>Population at risk</td><td>Total number of people who are exposed to a risk or set of risks responsible for a disease condition</td></tr><tr><td>15.</td><td>Prevalence</td><td>Number of old and new cases of a disease at a point in time</td></tr><tr><td>16.</td><td>Prognosis</td><td>Likely outcome of a disease condition</td></tr><tr><td>17.</td><td>Quarantine</td><td>A period of time that must elapse before those exposed to or attacked by a contagious disease can be considered as incapable respectively of developing or transmitting the disease.</td></tr><tr><td>18.</td><td>Sporadic</td><td>Is a disease that is normally absent from a population but which can occur in that population, although rarely and without predictable regularity.</td></tr><tr><td>19.</td><td>Study sample/population</td><td>Subset of the total population from whom information can be generated to reflect characteristics of the whole population</td></tr><tr><td>20.</td><td>Vital statistics</td><td>Record of import information about a population</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Define the following carriers – active, convalescent, healthy; incubatory and intermittent carriers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-0-sources-of-epidemiological-data">8.0 SOURCES OF EPIDEMIOLOGICAL DATA<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-0-sources-of-epidemiological-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Census</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>It is a process of collecting, compiling and publishing demographic, economic and social data pertaining at a specified time to all persons in a country.</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Registration of vital events (vital statistics)</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Registration of vital events keeps continuous check on demographic changes</li>
<li>Complete and accurate registration is a reliable source of health data</li>
<li>Vital events include legal registration, statistical recording and reporting of the occurrence of and the collection, compilation, presentation, analysis and distribution of statistics pertaining to vital events such as live births, deaths, foetal deaths, marriages, divorces, adoptions, separations,</li>
</ul>
<ol start="3">
<li>Notification</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Provides valuable information about fluctuation of disease frequency and early warning about new occurrences or outbreaks of disease</li>
<li>Notifiable diseases under the International Health regulation include cholera, plague and yellow fever. A few others such as Lassa fever, SARS, H1N1 influenza, etc. are placed under international surveillance</li>
</ul>
<ol start="4">
<li>Hospital records</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Data constitute a basic and primary source of information about disease prevalence</li>
<li>Provide information on geographic sources of patients, age and sex distribution of disease and duration of hospital stay, distribution if diagnosis, association between different diseases, period between disease and hospital admission, distribution of patient according different social and biological characteristics and cost of care.</li>
<li>Considered A poor guide to the estimation of disease frequency</li>
</ul>
<ol start="5">
<li>Disease registers</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Morbidity registers are a valuable source of information as to duration of illness, case fatality and survival</li>
<li>Registers allow follow up of patients and provide continuous account of frequency of disease in the absence of population base, useful information may be obtained from registers on natural course of disease.</li>
<li>It can provide data on morbidity from the particular diseases, treatment given and disease –specific mortality</li>
</ul>
<ol start="6">
<li>Record linkage</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>It is used to describe the process of bringing together records relating to one individual, the records originating in different times and places.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="7">
<li>Epidemiological surveillance</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Particular diseases are endemic, targeted for control, elimination and eradication.</li>
<li>Surveillance systems are set up to report on occurrence of new cases and efforts to control the diseases</li>
</ul>
<ol start="8">
<li>Active epidemiological surveillance</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>In-depth search for cases of a few selected diseases likely to cause epidemics e.g. sentinel surveillance for EPI diseases</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="9">
<li>Population surveys</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Surveys for evaluating health status of a population – community diagnosis and for investigation of factors affecting health and disease- environment, occupation</li>
<li>helpful for studying the natural history of disease, obtaining new information about disease aetiology and risk factors</li>
</ul>
<ol start="10">
<li>Computerized bibliographic database</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>DHS – Demographic Health Survey; DOLPHIN- Data online for Population, Health and Nutrition, GIDEON – Global Infectious Disease and Epidemiology Network</li>
</ul>
<p>Table 1.1: Advantages and disadvantages of different sources of data</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>Source</th><th>Advantages</th><th>Disadvantages</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>1.</td><td>Statistical Data</td><td>• Enables tracking of major trends</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Background information on broad social conditions</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Can go back to see trends</td><td>• Not always accessible</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Causes of death not known</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Incomplete statistics</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Is not fed back to role-players</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Not always reliable</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>2.</td><td>Hospital Clinic Records</td><td>• Regular, monitored, fits into overall system</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Basic information regarding community</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Information on individuals, families and communities</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Shows health trends</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• A statutory obligation to keep records</td><td>• Too much data (takes too long to collect</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Poor feedback &amp; communication</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Very seldom analysed.</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Single geographical area.</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Information from health sector only</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Only as good as record-keeper</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Only clinic visits are covered</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>3.</td><td>Other Departments Or Organisations</td><td>• Gives a comprehensive picture</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Gives inside story on community profile - political power, cultural influences</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Can provide information on perceived community needs</td><td>• Limited data (not easy to access)</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Unknown reliability</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Subjectivity</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Systems not compatible (age, standards)</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Inadequate training</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>4.</td><td>Health Information System</td><td>• Good source</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Linked up to other processes</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Can be interpreted locally.</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Can empower people</td><td>• Limited e.g. TB</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Questionable accuracy</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Needs intensive training of health worker</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Feedback and interpretation problematic</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Lack of incentive to analyse data locally</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>

<p>|  5. | Annual Reports | • Profile of needs and provision of services
• More textured than statistics
• Gives direction to other sources | • Poor analysis
• Biased and not always inclusive
• Too broad - summary
• Out of date due to lengthy production time
• Boring/not user friendly  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  6. | Internet | • Huge source, instant access to e.g. literature.
• Point of comparison e.g. international | • Limited facilities for access.
• Developing world less well represented.
• Not always up to date  |
|  7. | Text Books | • Expert information
• Identify broad range of problems
• Provides models/formats | • Out of date
• Not always applicable
• Too much text
• Theory does not inform implementation
• Costs/availability  |
|  8. | Research Projects | • Current information
• Time saving (if relevant)
• Can be specific to a problem
• Pilots/demonstration projects | • Costly and unsustainable.
• Not always applicable or relevant.
• Takes time (not often up to date)
• Author biased
• Creates expectations  |
|  9. | District Health Profiles | • Readily available
• Gives “bird’s eye” view
• Provides leads to other sources | • Questionable accuracy and reliability
• Questionable validity of out-dated profiles
• Takes time to keep updated  |
|  10. | Journals | • Current information
• Can get back numbers (old copies)
• Summarised.
• Different points of view | • Difficult to access
• Can be biased
• More academic than practical
• High volume  |
|  11. | Surveys | • Cost effective
• Focused/specific, pick up hidden information
• Can be spread over large areas e.g. national
• Correctly planned, much more comprehensive | • Once-off information
• Influenced by questionnaire
• Might not empower people  |
|  12. | Census | • Gives national picture
• Standardised denominators &amp; baseline data
• Useful for planning | • Not available until years later.
• Expensive.
• Creates expectations.
• Unknown reliability  |</p>
<p>9.0 PRACTICAL APPLICATION OF EPIDEMIOLOGY</p>
<ol>
<li>Evaluation of health services for baseline data, efficacy, effectiveness and efficiency</li>
<li>Policy formulation</li>
<li>To diagnose the health of the community and the condition of the people</li>
<li>To study the history of the health of populations, and of the rise and fall of diseases and changes in their character giving useful projections into the future</li>
<li>To study the working of health services with a view to their improvement</li>
<li>To estimate from the group experience what are the individual risks on average of disease, accident and defect, and the chances of avoiding them.</li>
<li>To identify syndromes by describing the distribution and association of clinical phenomena in the population.</li>
<li>To complete the clinical picture of chronic diseases and describe their natural history</li>
<li>To search for causes of health and disease by computing the experience of groups defined by their composition, inheritance and experience, their behaviour and environments</li>
</ol>
<p>10.0 DESCRIPTIVE VARIABLES FOR THE HEALTH OF THE COMMUNITY</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Demographic and social variables:
a) Age and sex distribution
b) Socioeconomic status
c) Family structure, including marital status and number of single parent families
d) Racial, ethnic, and religious composition</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Variables related to community infrastructure:
a) Availability of social and health services including hospitals and emergency rooms
b) Quality of housing stock including presence of lead-based paint and asbestos
c) Social stability (residential mobility)
d) Community policing
e) Employment opportunities</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Health-related outcome variables:
a) Homicide and suicide rates
b) Infant mortality rate
c) Mortality from selected conditions (cause specific)
d) Scope of chronic and infectious diseases
e) Alcoholism and substance abuse rates
f) Teenage pregnancy rates
g) Occurrence of sexually transmitted diseases
h) Birth rate</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Environmental variables:
a) Air pollution from stationary and mobile sources</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>b) Access to parks/recreational facilities
c) Availability of clean water
d) Availability of markets that supply healthful groceries
e) Number of liquor stores and fast-food outlets
f) Nutritional quality of foods and beverages vended to schoolchildren</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="11-0-ethical-and-professional-issues">11.0 ETHICAL AND PROFESSIONAL ISSUES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#11-0-ethical-and-professional-issues" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Include obligations to study subjects, privacy, confidentiality, access to data, race and ethnicity; conflict of interest and interpreting findings</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="12-0-clinical-medicine-vs-epidemiology">12.0 CLINICAL MEDICINE VS EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#12-0-clinical-medicine-vs-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Clinical descriptions of malaria: fever, diarrhoea, vomiting, headache.</li>
<li>Epidemiologic description: what age groups would be most likely affected, time trends, geographic trends and other variations that affect the distribution of malaria?</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classical-typical-epidemiology">Classical (Typical) Epidemiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classical-typical-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Population-oriented,</li>
<li>Studies the community origins of health problems, particularly those related to: nutrition, the environment, human behaviour and the psychologic, social and spiritual states of a population.</li>
<li>Classical epidemiologists are interested in discovering risk factors that might be altered/ modified in a population to prevent or delay disease or death.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-epidemiology">Clinical epidemiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Use similar research designs and statistical tools.</li>
<li>Science of making predictions about individual patients by counting clinical events in similar patients, using strong scientific methods for studies of groups of patients to ensure that predictions are accurate</li>
<li>Study patients in health care settings in order to improve diagnosis and treatment of various diseases and the prognosis of patients already affected by a disease.</li>
<li>Primary goal is to improve clinical decisions.</li>
<li>Some therefore prefer to call it clinical decision analysis</li>
</ul>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>Clinician</th><th>Epidemiologist</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>1.</td><td>Concerned with the health of an individual</td><td>Concerned with the health of individuals</td></tr><tr><td>2.</td><td>Looks for appropriate treatment for a disease</td><td>Looks for ways of preventing the disease</td></tr><tr><td>3.</td><td>Uses expensive and complicated equipment</td><td>Uses simple and cheap methods and equipment to study disease characteristics</td></tr><tr><td>4.</td><td>Concerned with a sick person</td><td>Concerned with the community</td></tr></tbody></table>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lesson-2-disease-determinants-and-prevention">Lesson 2: DISEASE - DETERMINANTS AND PREVENTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lesson-2-disease-determinants-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>At the end of the lesson the learner will be able to: -</p>
<ol>
<li>Discuss determinants of disease and concepts of immunity</li>
<li>Describe the natural history of a disease and the epidemiological triad</li>
<li>Discuss levels of disease prevention</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-0-disease">1.0 DISEASE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-0-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>What is disease?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-0-infection">2.0 INFECTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-0-infection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>What are the sources and causes of infection?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-0-process-of-infection">3.0 PROCESS OF INFECTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-0-process-of-infection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Six requirements for the successful invasion of the host by an infectious agent include</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Condition in the environment must be favourable to the agent or the agent must be able to adapt in the environment</li>
<li>Suitable reservoirs must be present</li>
<li>A susceptible host must be present</li>
<li>A satisfactory portal of entry into the host</li>
<li>Accessible portal of exit from the host</li>
<li>Appropriate means of dissemination and transmission to a new host</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-0-determinants-of-disease">4.0 DETERMINANTS OF DISEASE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-0-determinants-of-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Factors or events that are capable of bringing about change in health (PBSCB) i.e physical, biological, social, cultural and behavioural factors that influence health</li>
<li>Examples</li>
<li>Specific biologic agents (e.g., bacteria, viruses, protozoa).</li>
<li>Chemical agents that may act as carcinogens</li>
<li>Stress or adverse lifestyle patterns (lack of exercise, or tobacco consumption or diet high in saturated fat)</li>
<li>Determinants of disease can be intrinsic or extrinsic</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="intrinsic-determinants">Intrinsic Determinants<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intrinsic-determinants" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>What are the intrinsic and extrinsic determinants of disease?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="extrinsic-determinants">Extrinsic Determinants<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#extrinsic-determinants" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Physical - mechanical injuries/trauma, thermal, radiation, noise, psychological stress, light, etc.</li>
</ol>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>

<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Chemical - organic/inorganic forms, gases</li>
<li>Nutritional - metabolic, primary/secondary, nutritional deficiencies, hormonal imbalances, etc.</li>
<li>Biological - all living organisms that cause disease or infection - bacteria, virus, rickettsia, protozoa, fungi, etc. Genetic defects are included here</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-0-dynamics-of-disease-transmission">5.0 DYNAMICS OF DISEASE TRANSMISSION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-0-dynamics-of-disease-transmission" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Disease results from the interaction of the host (a person), the agent and the environment</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Many underlying principles govern transmission of diseases</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Human susceptibility is determined by genetic, nutritional and immunological characteristics</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Discuss the modes of disease transmission</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chain-of-transmission">Chain of Transmission<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chain-of-transmission" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It is a process that begins when (1) an infectious agent or pathogen (2) leaves its reservoir, source, or host through (3) a portal of exit, (4) is conveyed by some mode of transmission, (5) enters the host through an appropriate portal of entry, and (6) infects a susceptible host</li>
<li>The now-infected susceptible host becomes a new reservoir and the whole process starts over</li>
<li>The concept of a chain of infection is essential to our understanding of why we do what we do to prevent infection. If any link of the chain of infection can be broken, the spread of infection can be prevented.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-ec816534.jpeg" alt="img-1.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="factors-associated-with-increased-risk-of-human-disease">Factors associated with Increased Risk of Human Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#factors-associated-with-increased-risk-of-human-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>What are the factors associated with increased risk of human</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="6-0-concept-of-immunity">6.0 CONCEPT OF IMMUNITY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-0-concept-of-immunity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-0de07d9c.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="herd-immunity">HERD-IMMUNITY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#herd-immunity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Is the resistance of a group to an attack by a disease to which the large proportion of the members of the group are immune</li>
<li>Is the protective effect that arises from a high proportion of the population have had a disease (active natural immunization) or have been immunized (active artificial immunization)</li>
<li>Occurs when spread of disease in a community reaches a certain proportion of people who are immune in that the likelihood of encountering a susceptible individual to whom the disease can be transmitted is minimal</li>
<li>Important conditions for establishment of herd immunity include: -
a) Disease agent must be restricted to a single host species within which transmission occurs
b) Transmission must be relatively direct from one member of the host species to another
c) Infections must induce solid immunity
d) Probability of an infected person encountering every other individual in the population remains the same (constant random mixing of the population)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="7-0-host-parasite-relationships">7.0 HOST-PARASITE RELATIONSHIPS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-0-host-parasite-relationships" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Many microorganisms may benefit from mankind by developing a range of host-organism inter-relationship including</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Symbiosis is the cooperative association between two dissimilar organisms beneficial to both</li>
<li>Commensalism is an association between two dissimilar organisms living together benefiting one without harming the other</li>
<li>Parasitism is relation between two dissimilar organisms living together benefiting the parasite but harming the host</li>
<li>Saprophytism is a relation where organisms live on dead tissues.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="factors-determining-host-microorganisms-relationships">Factors Determining Host-Microorganisms Relationships<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#factors-determining-host-microorganisms-relationships" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Infectious agent factors
a. Mode of entry - Ingestion (external route), Inoculation (parenteral), Inhalation (respiratory) and Direct contact (contagious infection)
b. Spread of infection – through phagocytic cells, blood vessels, lymphatics
c. Production of toxin – exotoxins and endotoxins
d. Virulence of organisms (adhesions, penetration and colonization, toxins, antimicrobial resistance and host factors)
e. Products of organisms e.g. enzymes</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Host factors
a. Physical barrier e.g. skin
b. Chemical barrier – mucous secretions, gastric acid
c. Effective drainage in the respiratory tract, gastrointestinal tract, urinary and genital system
d. Immune defence mechanisms (immunity) - non-specific (innate) and specific or adaptive immune mechanisms (humoral – antibodies; cellular T and B cells)</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-0-natural-history-of-disease">8.0 NATURAL HISTORY OF DISEASE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-0-natural-history-of-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Every disease in a host follows a potentially predictable life cycle from onset to final outcome (natural history)</li>
<li>Understanding the natural history of disease is important to clinicians in establishing appropriate treatment and accurate prognosis, and it is vital to public health professionals in developing effective disease prevention and control strategies</li>
<li>Life cycle or natural history of a particular disease varies from individual to individual, and different diseases but four common stages manifest</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Stage of susceptibility</li>
<li>Stage of pre-symptomatic disease</li>
<li>Stage of clinical disease</li>
<li>Stage of diminished capacity/disability/death/chronicity</li>
</ol>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-efcf96b4.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg">
Diagram 2.1: Natural History of a Disease</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-stage-of-susceptibility">The stage of susceptibility<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-stage-of-susceptibility" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Precedes the onset of disease (disease has not yet developed)</li>
<li>Conditions necessary for disease development/occurrence exist (risk factors are present) making the host is susceptible</li>
<li>Examples</li>
<li>Individuals with high serum cholesterol, hypertension, a sedentary lifestyle, and diabetes, for example, have an increased risk of developing coronary heart disease</li>
<li>Lack of sleep, excessive stress, and poor eating habits may predispose one to the common cold</li>
<li>Susceptibility for kwashiorkor includes inadequate intake of proteins in diet or infectious diseases</li>
<li>Epidemiologists are continually seeking to identify and confirm risk factors for the major health problems that affect society</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stage-of-pre-symptomatic-disease">Stage of pre-symptomatic disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stage-of-pre-symptomatic-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Disease process has begun, but no overt signs or symptoms are evident to the host.</li>
<li>Characterized by symptoms that tentatively suggest that an individual is about to suffer from a given disease</li>
<li>For communicable diseases, this stage includes the incubation period, which is the time between the invasion of an infectious agent and the development of the first signs or symptoms of the disease</li>
<li>For non-communicable diseases the pathological process has begun but the signs and symptoms are not evident</li>
<li>A carrier of a communicable disease is an individual who has no symptoms of the disease but nevertheless harbours the causative agent</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="stage-of-clinical-disease">Stage of Clinical Disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stage-of-clinical-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Disease process is established and the signs and symptoms are evident following the pathological alteration in structure and function of the body tissues, organs and systems e.g. elevated blood pressure headache, fatigue in an individual with hypertension.</li>
<li>Most people seek medical treatment</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="stage-of-diminished-capacity-recovery-death-chronicity">Stage of Diminished Capacity/recovery/death/chronicity<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stage-of-diminished-capacity-recovery-death-chronicity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Revolves around effects on permanent damage caused by the disease process e.g. disabilities in hypertension, diabetes, syphilis</li>
<li>If disease is not treated it may cause disability, become chronic or lead to death</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="assignment">ASSIGNMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#assignment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Discuss the natural history of the following diseases – tuberculosis; Diabetes mellitus; Hypertension; HIV; Syphilis; Pneumonia; Amoebiasis; Tetanus; Poliomyelitis; Rheumatic fever</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-77d26125.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg">
Diagram 2.2: Stages of a Disease</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-f88549cc.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg">
The Stages of a Disease</p>

<p>17</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="9-0-epidemiological-triad-host-agent-environment-model">9.0 EPIDEMIOLOGICAL TRIAD (HOST-AGENT-ENVIRONMENT MODEL)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#9-0-epidemiological-triad-host-agent-environment-model" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Amount of disease in a population results from interaction of the 3 components</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>In a state of equilibrium the host and the agent are in a state of balance in the environment and there is no disease</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>When a change occurs that favours the existence and multiplication of the agent disease frequency increases</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If a change favours the host such as improved nutrition disease frequency decreases</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The epidemiological triad/triangle comprises of : -</p>
</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>A susceptible host (the person at risk for the disease)</li>
<li>A disease agent (the proximate cause)</li>
<li>An environmental context for the interaction between the host and agent</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-host">The Host<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-host" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The individual human being whose state is determined by the interaction of genetics and environmental factors</li>
<li>Disease only occurs in a host who is susceptible (lack of susceptibility is due to immunity or inherent factors¹)</li>
<li>Examples: age, sex, ethnic groups, nutritional status, socio-economic, personal behaviours (smoking, diet, drinking, exercise, sexual behaviours), personality, immunization status and physical states – pregnancy, puberty, fatigue, immunocompromised</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-3b79977c.jpeg" alt="img-6.jpeg">
Diagram 2.3: Epidemiologic Triad</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="agent">Agent<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#agent" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>HIV subtype (A, B, C, D, E)</li>
<li>Phenotypic differences</li>
<li>Genotypic differences</li>
<li>Antiretroviral drug resistance</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="environment">Environment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#environment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Social norms</li>
<li>Avg rate of sex-partner change</li>
<li>Local prevalence/probability of exposure</li>
<li>Social and economic determinants or risk behavior</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-agent">The Agent<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-agent" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>This is the factor whose presence or absence causes a disease</li>
<li>Examples - biological agents, chemical agents, nutritive element and physical agents</li>
</ul>
<p>¹ Ability to resist diseases due to factors other than antibodies e.g. good nutrition, exercises</p>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="environmental-factors">Environmental factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#environmental-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Physical – weather, climate, geology</li>
<li>Biological – sources of food, water and air, presence of vectors, flora and fauna</li>
<li>Socioeconomic and cultural – density, crowding, adequate housing, war, sanitation and availability of health care</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="10-0-levels-of-disease-prevention">10.0 LEVELS OF DISEASE PREVENTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#10-0-levels-of-disease-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Disease prevention applies to measures taken to prevent diseases before they occur as well as measures taken to prevent disease progression</li>
<li>There are four levels of disease prevention namely primordial, primary, secondary and tertiary</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="primordial-level">Primordial Level<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#primordial-level" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Primordial prevention is defined as prevention of risk factors themselves, beginning with change in social and environmental conditions in which these factors are observed to develop, and continuing for high risk children, adolescents and young adults</li>
<li>Examples of primordial prevention actions - National policies and programmes on nutrition involving the agricultural sector, the food industry, and the food import-export sector</li>
<li>Responsibility lies on the government. professional and non-governmental organizations, industry, hospitals, health clinics, health practitioners and health-care workers</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-2ec7013f.jpeg" alt="img-7.jpeg"></p>
<p>Diagram 2.4: Levels of Disease Prevention</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="primary-level-primary-intervention">Primary Level (Primary Intervention)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#primary-level-primary-intervention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Entails measures taken to prevent diseases before they occur</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Strategies emphasize general health promotion, risk factor reduction, and other health protective measures</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Strategies include: -</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Health education and health promotion programs designed to foster healthier lifestyles and environmental health programs designed to improve environmental quality</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Specific examples of primary prevention measures include immunization against communicable diseases; public health education about good nutrition, exercise, stress management, and individual responsibility for health; chlorination and filtration of public water supplies; and legislation requiring child restraints in motor vehicles.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Examples</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>General health promotion strategies taken at home, working places and in institutions e.g. promotion of good nutrition, provision of basic needs (food, clothing, shelter), recreation facilities</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Specific measures - immunization, avoidance of substances (e.g. drugs), prevention of accidents</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>What strategies has the government employed to facilitate primary intervention? Discuss using specific examples</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="secondary-level-curative-level">Secondary Level (Curative level)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#secondary-level-curative-level" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Entails early detection of disease followed by prompt intervention</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is important in preventing complications, disabilities and communicability of disease</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Secondary prevention focuses on early detection and swift treatment of disease</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Its purpose is to cure disease, slow its progression, or reduce its impact on individuals or communities</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A common approach to secondary prevention is screening for disease</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Examples</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Screening - non-invasive computerized test for the early detection of heart disease, mMammography for breast cancer detection; eye tests for glaucoma; blood tests for lead exposure; occult blood tests for colorectal cancer; the Pap test for cervical concrete breath test for Helicobacter pylori, the bacterium implicated in duodenal and gastric ulcers; and the Prostate-Specific Antigen(PSA) test for prostate cancer</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Treatment e.g. treatment of hypertension to prevent complications and removal of skin cancer lesions as they occur</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Protects healthy members of the community against certain diseases</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>What strategies has the government employed to facilitate secondary/curative intervention? Discuss using specific examples</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="tertiary-level-rehabilitative-intervention">Tertiary Level (Rehabilitative Intervention)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tertiary-level-rehabilitative-intervention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Consists of various attempts made to improve the quality of life of an individual after a disease has occurred and caused damage to the person</li>
<li>Involves both therapeutic and rehabilitative measures once disease is firmly established</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
5
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Examples include treatment of diabetics to prevent complications; management of chronic heart disease patients with medication, diet, exercise, and periodic examination; improving functioning of stroke patients through rehabilitation by occupational and physical therapy, nursing care, speech therapy, counselling, and so forth, and treating those suffering from complications of diseases such as meningitis, multiple sclerosis, or Parkinson's disease.</li>
<li>May involve modification of working and home environments and funding affected persons to start IGAs</li>
</ul>
<p>What strategies has the government employed to facilitate tertiary/rehabilitative intervention? Discuss using specific examples</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="11-0-classification-of-diseases">11.0 CLASSIFICATION OF DISEASES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#11-0-classification-of-diseases" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-communicable-diseases">1) Communicable Diseases<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-communicable-diseases" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Communicable diseases are transmissible from one person, or animal, to another</li>
<li>A communicable disease is defined as an illness that arises from transmission of an infectious agent or its toxic product from an infected person, animal or reservoir to a susceptible host, either directly or indirectly through an intermediate plant or animal host, vector, or environment.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-non-communicable-diseases">2) Non-Communicable Diseases<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-non-communicable-diseases" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Non-communicable diseases (NCDs) are chronic medical conditions or diseases which are non-infectious</li>
<li>NCDs are currently responsible for over 60% of global deaths</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="12-0-disease-outbreak">12.0 DISEASE OUTBREAK<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#12-0-disease-outbreak" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A disease outbreak is the occurrence of a number of cases of a disease that is larger than expected for a given time and place.</li>
<li>Outbreaks are classified according to the mode of spread (i.e. point source of propagation) and major clinical presenting features.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="10-steps-for-investigation-of-disease-outbreak">10 STEPS for Investigation of Disease Outbreak<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#10-steps-for-investigation-of-disease-outbreak" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Prepare to investigate
a) Identify outbreak investigation team
b) Review scientific literature
c) Notify appropriate state and local entities
d) Determine if immediate control measures are needed</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Verify the diagnosis and confirm outbreak
a) Get laboratory confirmation
b) Collect stool specimens from ill persons</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>c) Perform bacteriologic, virologic or parasitic testing at the Georgia Public Health Laboratory (GPHL) Link patients and environmental specimens by DNA fingerprinting/Pulse Field Gel Electrophoresis (PFGE)</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>
<p>Case definition
a) Establish a set of standard criteria for deciding who are the ill persons related to the outbreak ("case-patients")
b) Narrow or broad (confirm, probable, suspect)
c) DYNAMIC: may change during investigation</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Case finding
a) Conduct systematic search based on case definition
b) Create line list of possible cases (people exposed)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Perform descriptive epidemiology
a) Tabulate and orient data: person, place, time
b) Frequencies
c) Mapping
d) Epidemic Curve</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Hypothesis generation—the how and the why
a) Compare with known sources or similar outbreaks
b) Design questionnaire</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Evaluate hypothesis thorough statistics
a) Perform epidemiologic study: cohort, case-control
b) Compare risk factors among ill (cases) vs not ill (controls)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Additional environmental studies
a) Collect food, water, and/or environmental samples
b) Determine what happened with the implicated source or food</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Implement control/prevention measure
a) Coordinate with all stakeholders including regulatory partners
b) Develop strategies to prevent further or future illness</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Communicate findings
a) Disseminate outbreak investigation report—internal and external audience
b) Educate community, ill persons, restaurant staff, and Public Health Staff</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>TASK
Explain how the following influence disease transmission - herd immunity, incubation period, attack rate, carrier state and epidemics</p>
