<li>Case reports and case series are most useful for describing the potential effectiveness of new interventions, for describing the effectiveness of interventions on unusual diagnoses, and for describing unusual responses (either good or bad) to interventions</li>
<li>Case series are often conducted prospectively</li>
<li>Primary distinction between case reports/series and the single-subject experiment is that the researcher does not manipulate the intervention in a case report/series but merely describes/documents what happened during the normal course of the intervention.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="case-series">CASE SERIES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#case-series" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Collections of individual case reports</li>
<li>May occur in a relatively short time period</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Can indicate the beginning or presence of an epidemic</li>
<li>Hypothesis formulation - through investigation of the experiences of the affected individuals</li>
<li>Identification of possible causal factors – analytic study to compare experiences of the case series with a group of individuals who did not develop the disease</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Clinical case-series are of value in epidemiology for:
i) Studying symptoms and signs
ii) Creating case definitions
iii) Clinical education, audit and research</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Population based</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>When a clinical case-series is complete for a defined geographical area for which the population is known, it is, effectively, a population based case-series consisting of a population register of cases.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Epidemiologically the most important case-series are registers of serious diseases or deaths, and of health service utilisation, e.g. hospital admissions.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Usually compiled for administrative and legal reasons.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Population</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Full epidemiological use of case-series data needs information on the population to permit calculation of rates</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Key to understanding the distribution of disease in populations and to the study of variations over time, between places and by population characteristics</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Case-series can provide the key to sound case control and cohort studies and trials</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="requirements-for-interpretation">Requirements for interpretation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#requirements-for-interpretation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To make sense of case-series data the key requirements are:
i) The diagnosis (case definition) or, for mortality, the cause of death
ii) The date when the disease or death occurred (time)
iii) The place where the person lived, worked etc. (place)</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>iv) The characteristics of the person (person)
v) The opportunity to collect additional data from medical records (possibly by electronic data linkage) or the person directly
vi) The size and characteristics of the population at risk
vii) Case-series data can be linked to other health data either in the past or the future, e.g. mortality data can be linked to hospital admissions including at birth and childhood, cancer registrations and other records to obtain information on exposures and disease.
viii) Cases may also be contacted for additional information.
ix) This type of action may turn a case-series design into a cohort design.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advantages">Advantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Population case-series permit two arguably unique forms of epidemiological analysis and insight</li>
<li>Paint a truly national and even international population perspective on disease.</li>
<li>The disease patterns can be related to aspects of society or the environment that affect the population but have no sensible measure at the individual level e.g. ozone concentration at ground level and the thickness of the ozone layer in the earth's atmosphere.</li>
<li>Useful in the formulation of research hypotheses – suggestive of risk factors</li>
<li>Important step in recognizing new diseases or risk factors</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disadvantages">Disadvantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disadvantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Case report is based on the experience of one individual – The presence of any "risk factor" may be coincidental</li>
<li>Can't use to test for valid statistical association (No comparison group)</li>
<li>Can merely raise the question of an association</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-1-3-cross-sectional-designs-community-health-studies-surveys">4.1.3. CROSS SECTIONAL DESIGNS (Community health studies, surveys)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-1-3-cross-sectional-designs-community-health-studies-surveys" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Is an observational study of a defined population at a single point in time or over a defined period of time</li>
<li>Used to determine the prevalence of disease in the community</li>
<li>In a cross-sectional study prevalent (existing) cases are identified rather than incident (new) cases</li>
<li>Prevalent cases may not be representative of all cases in this population</li>
<li>Often generate descriptive information on the prevalence of health outcomes or determinants of health</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advantages">Advantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Convenient and inexpensive, economic</li>
</ol>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
35
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Quick</li>
<li>Can consider several exposures and several diseases/allows study of several diseases</li>
<li>Can generate hypotheses</li>
<li>Usually represents the general population</li>
<li>Useful for estimation of the population burden, health planning and priority setting of health problems</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disadvantages">Disadvantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disadvantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Cannot establish whether the exposure preceded disease or disease influenced exposure</li>
<li>Can identify only prevalent cases rather than incident cases</li>
<li>Possible bias since only survivors are available for study</li>
<li>May under-represent diseases with short duration</li>
<li>Liable to survivor bias</li>
<li>Possible measurement error;</li>
<li>Not suitable for rare conditions;</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-2-analytic-designs">4.2. ANALYTIC DESIGNS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-2-analytic-designs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Focus on searching for the underlying causes with the main purpose being to uncover the source and mode of spread of disease</li>
<li>Are designed specifically to test hypotheses that have usually been generated from descriptive studies</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-2-1-longitudinal-cohort-studies">4.2.1. LONGITUDINAL COHORT STUDIES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-2-1-longitudinal-cohort-studies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Are observational where the goal of the investigator is to observe outcomes as they occur, rather than to experimentally manipulate outcomes by applying an intervention</li>
<li>Begins by identifying a group of people who are initially free of the outcome of interest, but who vary in terms of their degree of exposure to various factors that may cause or prevent the outcome</li>
<li>Subjects are then followed over time to determine whether the outcome of interest occurs</li>
<li>Although cohort studies are observational, they can sometimes offer strong evidence about the effectiveness of an intervention, particularly when it is not ethical or efficient to randomize subjects to different therapies</li>
<li>Involves data collection from different sample at each data collection point in a population that is constant e.g. checking dropout rate in schools</li>
<li>Cohort studies</li>
<li>i) Can be large or small</li>
<li>ii) Can be long or short</li>
<li>iii) Can be simple or elaborate</li>
<li>iv) Can be local or multinational</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>v) For rare outcomes need many people and/or lengthy follow-up
vi) May have to decide what characteristics to measure long in advance</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-264b1923.jpeg" alt="img-14.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="intuitive-approach-to-studying-disease-incidence-and-risk-factors">Intuitive approach to studying disease incidence and risk factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intuitive-approach-to-studying-disease-incidence-and-risk-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Start with a population at risk</li>
<li>Measure characteristics at baseline</li>
<li>Follow-up the population over time with surveillance or re-examination</li>
<li>Compare event rates in people with and without characteristics of interest</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="assumptions">Assumptions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#assumptions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Exposed and non-exposed groups are representative of a well-defined general population</li>
<li>Absence of exposure well defined</li>
<li>Outcome assessment comparable between exposed and non-exposed</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advantages">Advantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Can establish population-based incidence</li>
<li>Accurate relative risk (risk ratio) estimation</li>
<li>Can examine rare exposures (asbestos &gt; lung cancer)</li>
<li>Temporal relationship can be inferred (prospective design)</li>
<li>Time-to-event analysis is possible</li>
<li>Can be used where randomization is not possible</li>
<li>Magnitude of a risk factor's effect can be quantified</li>
<li>Selection and information biases are decreased</li>
<li>Multiple outcomes can be studied (smoking &gt; lung cancer, COPD, larynx cancer)</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disadvantages">Disadvantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disadvantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Lengthy and expensive</li>
<li>May require very large samples</li>
<li>Not suitable for rare diseases</li>
<li>Not suitable for diseases with long-latency</li>
<li>Unexpected environmental changes may influence the association</li>
</ol>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>37</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>Nonresponse, migration and loss-to-follow-up biases</li>
<li>Sampling, ascertainment and observer biases are still possible</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="retrospective-cohort-designs">Retrospective Cohort Designs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#retrospective-cohort-designs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Also called historical cohort; non-concurrent prospective cohort</li>
<li>The cohort is identified from past records and followed from the time of those records up to some defined point in the recent past or up to the present time</li>
<li>Study outcomes are assessed at this defined point and the investigator goes back in time to determine the subjects' exposures and risk factor characteristics</li>
<li>An investigator accesses a historical roster of all exposed and non-exposed persons and then determines their current case/non-case status</li>
<li>The investigator initiates the study when the disease is already established in the cohort of individuals, long after the measurement of exposure</li>
<li>Requires sound data on exposure status for both cases and non-cases at a designated earlier time-point</li>
<li>Investigator</li>
<li>uses existing data collected in the past to identify the population and the exposure status (exposed/not exposed groups)</li>
<li>Determines at present the (development) status of disease</li>
<li>Investigator spends a relatively short time to assemble study population (and the exposed/not exposed groups) from past data</li>
<li>Determine disease status at the present time (no future follow-up)</li>
<li>Potential problems</li>
<li>i) Selection bias</li>
<li>ii) Misclassification</li>
<li>Advantages</li>
<li>i) Can be useful when reliable records are available</li>
<li>ii) Investigators can determine the case status of the entire group at the present time, then use the exposure records to assess the relationship between exposure and disease</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prospective-cohort-designs">Prospective Cohort Designs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prospective-cohort-designs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The cohort is assembled in the present and followed into the future</li>
<li>Investigator starts the study (from the beginning) with the identification of the population and the exposure status (exposed/not exposed groups)</li>
<li>Follows them (over time) for the development of disease</li>
<li>Study outcomes are recorded after baseline characteristics of subjects have been assessed</li>
<li>Takes a relatively long time to complete the study (as long as the length of the study). Study outcomes are recorded after baseline characteristics of subjects have been assessed.</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="bias">BIAS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bias" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Selection bias - select participants into exposed and not exposed groups based on some characteristics that may affect the outcome</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Information bias
a. Collect different quality and extent of information from exposed and not exposed groups
b. Loss to follow-up differs between exposed and not exposed (or between disease and no disease)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Misclassification bias - misclassify exposure status or disease status</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-9e1b4b82.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-2-2-case-controlled-studies">4.2.2 CASE CONTROLLED STUDIES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-2-2-case-controlled-studies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Identify participants based on their disease/outcome status and compare presence of risk factor</li>
<li>Subjects who are known to have the outcome of interest are first identified as cases, and then a comparison(control) group, is assembled</li>
<li>Both groups are then evaluated and the distribution of past exposures, risk factors, and/or subject characteristics in the case and control groups are compared</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-85258340.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg"></p>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>In a retrospective case-control study</li>
<li>Assessment of the exposure or risk factor occurs after subjects are classified as cases or controls</li>
<li>In a prospective case-control study</li>
<li>All measurements of the exposure or risk factor variables are recorded before subjects are classified as cases or controls</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="assumptions">Assumptions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#assumptions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Cases representative of all cases of disease</li>
<li>Controls drawn from the same population as cases (and at risk for the outcome)</li>
<li>Exposure data collected similarly in cases and controls</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="case-selection">Case Selection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#case-selection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Cases are identified on the basis of their disease/phenotype, representative of all individuals who develop disease</li>
<li>Distinguishing incident from prevalent or recurrent cases important</li>
<li>High participant rates important</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="controls-selection">Controls Selection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#controls-selection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Controls are representative of the general population who do not develop the disease</li>
<li>Selected from population at risk to become case</li>
<li>Families, population registries, neighbourhood</li>
<li>Who is the population at risk?</li>
<li>How do you know they don't have the disease?</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="examples">Examples<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#examples" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Aspirin and Reye's syndrome in children</li>
<li>Oral contraceptives and reduced risk of ovarian/endometrial cancer</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advantages">Advantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Cheap, easy and quick studies</li>
<li>Multiple exposures can be examined</li>
<li>Rare diseases and diseases with long latency can be studied</li>
<li>Suitable when randomization is unethical (alcohol and pregnancy outcome)</li>
<li>Suitable for rare outcomes</li>
<li>Suitable for outcomes with long induction period</li>
<li>Need fewer people in some cases</li>
<li>Readily evaluate multiple exposures</li>
<li>Convenient</li>
</ol>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Disadvantages</p>
<ol>
<li>Case and control selection troublesome</li>
<li>Subject to bias (selection, recall, misclassification)</li>
<li>Direct incidence estimation is not possible</li>
<li>Temporal relationship is not clear</li>
<li>Multiple outcomes cannot be studied</li>
<li>If the incidence of exposure is high, it is difficult to show the difference between cases and controls</li>
<li>Not easy to estimate attributable fraction</li>
<li>Reverse causation is a problem in interpretation - especially in molecular epidemiology studies</li>
</ol>
<p>5.0. EXPERIMENTAL DESIGNS</p>
<ul>
<li>A population is selected for a planned trial of a regimen, whose effects are measured by comparing the outcome of the regimen in the experimental group versus the outcome of another regimen in the control group</li>
<li>Such designs are differentiated from observational designs by the fact that there is manipulation of the study factor (exposure), and randomization (random allocation) of subjects to treatment (exposure) groups.</li>
<li>Investigator studies the effect of a factor under his/her control by determining which group to expose to the factor under study</li>
</ul>
<p>WHY PERFORMED</p>
<ol>
<li>Provide stronger evidence of the effect (outcome) compared to observational designs, with maximum confidence and assurance</li>
<li>Yield more valid results, as variation is minimized and bias controlled</li>
<li>Determine whether experimental treatments are safe and effective under "controlled environments" (as opposed to "natural settings" in observational designs), especially when the margin of expected benefit is doubtful / narrow (10 - 30%)</li>
</ol>
<p>5.1 COMMUNITY TRIALS</p>
<ul>
<li>Experiments involving communities as a whole are called community trials</li>
<li>The group as a whole is collectively studied as opposed to clinical trials where an individual within a group is studied</li>
<li>In a community trial,</li>
<li>Investigator selects two communities that have very similar characteristics</li>
<li>Obtains community assent from leaders</li>
<li>Conducts a survey in each community to measure incidence and prevalence of the disease of interest and prevalence of suspected risk factors</li>
<li>Introduce interventions</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>o Conduct a survey
o Note the net difference</p>
<ul>
<li>Example - introduction of fluorides into water supply in order to determine whether it decreases frequency of dental caries</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="conducting-a-community-trials">Conducting a community trials<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#conducting-a-community-trials" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="six-steps">Six steps<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#six-steps" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Development of a protocol</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>State the rationale, procedures and organization</li>
<li>Detailed description of methods of assessment</li>
<li>Methods of data analysis</li>
<li>Provide contingences</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Community selection and recruitment</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Communities should be similar in as many aspects as possible</li>
<li>Select interventions and control</li>
<li>Determine the number and size of the communities</li>
<li>Selected communities should be stable with little migration</li>
<li>Based on 5 criteria</li>
<li>i) Unusual prevalence of the disease</li>
<li>ii) Unusual prevalence of suspected risk factors</li>
<li>iii) Administrative convenience</li>
<li>iv) Favourable community relations</li>
<li>v) Availability of background demographic information</li>
<li>Recruitment involves</li>
<li>i) Obtain consent from leaders, officials and health professionals</li>
<li>ii) Inform community members about the study – newspapers, local radio and TV stations, barazas</li>
</ul>
<ol start="3">
<li>Establishment of a baseline and community surveillance</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Examples - changes in mortality rates, incidence of disease and changes in risk factors for disease</li>
<li>Once an investigator selects outcomes of interest then he/she must select the baseline for the outcomes</li>
<li>Community surveillance (6 steps)</li>
<li>i) Development of a protocol – solicit community support</li>
<li>ii) Community outreach</li>
<li>iii) Establish a diagnostic criteria</li>
<li>iv) Ascertainment of cases</li>
<li>v) Validation</li>
<li>vi) Data management</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Intervention selection and assignment</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Specify the intervention to be used e.g. education</li>
<li>If the intervention has any injurious effect it is has to be stopped</li>
<li>Data collection also allows surveillance</li>
</ul>
<ol start="5">
<li>Oversight and data monitoring (involves data collection and analysis)</li>
<li>Evaluation (data analysis and making inferences and conclusions)</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="randomized-community-trials">Randomized Community Trials<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#randomized-community-trials" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Communities are selected using simple random sampling</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="non-randomized-community-trials">Non-Randomized Community Trials<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-randomized-community-trials" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Communities are selected by non-probability methods</li>
<li>An element of bias is possible to ensue</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-2-clinical-trials">5.2 CLINICAL TRIALS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-2-clinical-trials" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Is an experiment that evaluates the effects of one or more therapeutic interventions in groups of human subjects</li>
<li>Clinical trials - an individual within a group is studied</li>
<li>Effect of an intervention is evaluated by comparing a group of subjects receiving a standard intervention to a group receiving a new intervention. If no standard intervention exists, then a control group receiving no intervention or a placebo intervention can be used as the comparison.</li>
<li>Assignment to a given intervention group may be random, as when a coin is tossed or a random number generator is used, or it may be entirely non-random.</li>
<li>Random assignment or randomization improves the ability to attribute differences between groups to differences in the types of interventions received. Although randomization is preferable because it helps to control for factors other than the intervention that may be responsible for differences in outcomes, non-randomized assignment may be necessary when randomization is not feasible or ethical.</li>
<li>Additionally, when little evidence is available to justify the difficulty and expense of a fully-randomized clinical trial, a non-randomized clinical trial might be the first step in that direction.</li>
<li>Non-randomized clinical trials are sometimes referred to as "quasi-experimental" clinical trials or "non-equivalent control group" designs because the characteristics of subjects in non-randomized groups will tend to be non-equivalent. The estimation of intervention effects in non-randomized clinical trials may be biased if group differences in subject characteristics are not controlled for in the data analysis</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="randomized-clinical-trials">Randomized Clinical Trials<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#randomized-clinical-trials" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>A comparative study in which study subjects are assigned by a formal chance mechanism between two or more intervention strategies (gold standard for inferring causality)</li>
<li>Also called "randomized controlled trials," experimental studies</li>
<li>Participant assigned to intervention group by a formal chance mechanism</li>
<li>Assumptions</li>
<li>Exposure must be potentially modifiable</li>
<li>Primary outcomes are relatively common, occur relatively soon</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-2a50c769.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="methods-of-randomization">Methods of randomization<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#methods-of-randomization" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Several choices, from "flipping a coin" to stratified randomization</li>
<li>Blinding/masking</li>
<li>Participant, study investigator (and anybody else involved in follow-up)</li>
<li>Ideally, double-blinded</li>
<li>Analysis: intention-to-treat</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="advantages">Advantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Similar distribution of baseline characteristics in comparison groups</li>
<li>Protection against confounders, both known and unknown</li>
<li>Able to directly estimate risk</li>
<li>Allows comparison of multiple outcomes</li>
<li>Best evidence study design</li>
<li>No inclusion bias (using blinding)</li>
<li>Controlling for possible confounders</li>
<li>Comparable Groups (using randomization)</li>
</ol>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="disadvantages">Disadvantages<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disadvantages" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Limitations on types of interventions</li>
<li>Costly</li>
<li>Not suitable for rare outcomes</li>
<li>Not suitable for outcomes requiring long or extensive follow-up</li>
<li>Potential challenges to the generalizability of findings</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Eligibility: strict inclusion/exclusion</li>
<li>Adherence/withdrawal issues</li>
</ul>
<ol start="4">
<li>Large trials (may affect statistical power)</li>
<li>Long term follow-up (possible losses)</li>
<li>Compliance</li>
<li>Expensive</li>
<li>Public health perspective?</li>
<li>Possible ethical questions</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="non-randomized-studies">Non-Randomized Studies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-randomized-studies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A clinical trial in which the participants are not assigned by chance to different treatment groups. Participants may choose which group they want to be in, or they may be assigned to the groups by the researchers.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-f38dd65d.jpeg" alt="img-18.jpeg"></p>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
5
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lesson-6-sampling-methods-and-data-analysis-in-epidemiology">Lesson 6: SAMPLING METHODS AND DATA ANALYSIS IN EPIDEMIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lesson-6-sampling-methods-and-data-analysis-in-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>At the end of the lesson the learner will be able to: -</p>
<ol>
<li>Define key words</li>
<li>Describe methods of sampling in epidemiological studies</li>
<li>Describe methods of data analysis and presentation in epidemiological studies</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sampling-methods">SAMPLING METHODS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sampling-methods" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-0-introduction">1.0. INTRODUCTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-0-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Sampling is the process or technique of selecting a sample of appropriate characteristics and adequate size.</li>
<li>Definitions</li>
<li>i) A population is defined by:</li>
<li>Its nature (an individual, housing, a firm etc.)</li>
<li>Its intrinsic characteristics (gender, housing type, industries)</li>
<li>Its localisation (city, neighbourhood etc.)</li>
<li>who, where and when</li>
<li>ii) Sampling unit – the basic unit around which a sampling procedure is planned e.g., a person, group (household, school, district, etc.) or a component (e.g. eye, physiological response)</li>
<li>iii) Sampling frame – list of all of the sampling units in a population</li>
<li>iv) Sample – collection of sampling units from the eligible population</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-43a9c632.jpeg" alt="img-19.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-0-sampling-methods">2.0. SAMPLING METHODS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-0-sampling-methods" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Random (Probability) Sampling
a) Simple random sample
b) Stratified random sample
c) Cluster sample
d) Adaptive cluster sample
e) Multistage sample</li>
</ol>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Non-random Sample
a) Convenience sample
b) Systematic sample
c) Consecutive sample
d) Quota sample
e) Volunteer sample
f) Capture-recapture</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-1-probability-sampling-methods">2.1. PROBABILITY SAMPLING METHODS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-1-probability-sampling-methods" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-simple-random-sampling">1) Simple Random sampling<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-simple-random-sampling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Simple random sampling without replacement a simple random sample is one in which each of the possible samples of elements taken from a population of elements has the same probability of selection</li>
<li>In a simple random sample without replacement, any element selected in a sample cannot be selected again for the same sample</li>
<li>Advantages
i) Simple process and easy to understand
ii) Easy calculation of means and variance
iii) Sampling error easily measured</li>
<li>Disadvantages
i) Not most efficient method, that is, not the most precise estimate for the cost
ii) Requires knowledge of the complete sampling frame
iii) Cannot always be certain that there is an equal chance of selection
iv) Non respondents or refusals</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-systematic-sampling">2) Systematic sampling<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-systematic-sampling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Sampling units are spaced regularly throughout the sampling frame, e.g., every 3rd unit would be selected</li>
<li>May be used as either probability sample or not</li>
<li>Not a probability sample unless the starting point is randomly selected</li>
<li>Non-random sample if the starting point is determined by some other mechanism than chance</li>
<li>Principle - Select sampling units at regular intervals (e.g. every 20th unit)</li>
<li>Procedure</li>
<li>Arrange the units in some kind of sequence</li>
<li>Divide total sampling population by the designated sample size (e.g. 1200/60=20)</li>
<li>Choose a random starting point (for 20, the starting point will be a random number between 1 and 20)</li>
<li>Select units at regular intervals (in this case, every 20th unit), i.e. 4th, 24th, 44th etc.</li>
<li>Advantages
i) Sampling frame does not need to be defined in advance</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>ii) Easier to implement in the field
iii) If there are unrecognized trends in the sample frame, systematic sample ensure coverage of the spectrum of units</p>
<ul>
<li>Disadvantages
i) Variance cannot be estimated unless assumptions are made</li>
<li>Example: Estimate HIV prevalence in children born during a specified period at a hospital</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-stratified-random-sample">3) Stratified random sample<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-stratified-random-sample" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>The sampling frame comprises groups, or strata, with certain characteristics</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A sample of units are selected from each group or stratum</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Used for a population with distinct subgroups</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Procedure</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Divide (stratify) sampling frame into homogeneous subgroups (strata) e.g. age-group, urban/rural areas, regions, occupations</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Draw random sample within each stratum</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Advantages
i) Assures that certain subgroups are represented in a sample
ii) Allows investigator to estimate parameters in different strata
iii) More precise estimates of the parameters because strata are more homogeneous, e.g., smaller variance within strata
iv) Strata of interest can be sampled most intensively, e.g., groups with greatest variance
v) Administrative advantages</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Disadvantages
i) Loss of precision if small number of units is sampled from strata
ii) Sampling error is difficult to measure
iii) Different strata can be difficult to identify
iv) Loss of precision if small numbers in individual strata (resolved by sampling proportional to stratum population)</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-cluster-sampling">4) Cluster sampling<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-cluster-sampling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Clusters of sampling units are first selected randomly</li>
<li>Individual sampling units are then selected from within each cluster</li>
<li>Principle</li>
<li>Whole population divided into groups e.g. neighbourhoods</li>
<li>A type of multi-stage sampling where all units at the lower level are included in the sample</li>
<li>Random sample taken of these groups ("clusters")</li>
<li>Within selected clusters, all units e.g. households included (or random sample of these units)</li>
<li>Provides logistical advantage</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Advantages
i) The entire sampling frame need not be enumerated in advance, just the clusters once identified
ii) More economical in terms of resources than simple random sampling</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Disadvantages
i) Loss of precision, i.e., wider variance, but can be accounted for with larger number of clusters</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Example: Estimate the prevalence of dental caries in school children</p>
</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Among the schools in the catchments area, list all of the classrooms in each school</li>
<li>Take a simple random sample of classrooms, or cluster of children</li>
<li>Examine all children in a cluster for dental caries</li>
<li>Estimate prevalence of caries within clusters than combine in overall estimate, with variance</li>
</ol>
<ol start="5">
<li>Multistage sampling</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Similar to cluster sampling except that there are two sampling events, instead of one</li>
<li>Primary units are randomly selected</li>
<li>Individual units within primary units randomly selected for measurement</li>
<li>Example: Estimate the prevalence of dental caries in school children</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Among the schools in the catchment area, list all of the classrooms in each school</li>
<li>Take a simple random sample of classrooms, or cluster of children</li>
<li>Enumerate the children in each classroom</li>
<li>Take a simple random sample of children within the classroom</li>
<li>Examine all children in a cluster for dental caries</li>
<li>Estimate prevalence of caries within clusters than combine in overall estimate, with variance</li>
</ol>
<p>2.2 NON-PROBABILITY SAMPLING METHODS</p>
<ol>
<li>Convenience (Accidental, Haphazard) Sampling</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>
<p>A non-random collection of sampling units from an undefined sampling frame</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Advantages
i) Convenient and easy to perform</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Disadvantages
i) Not statistical justification for sample
ii) Biased</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Examples - case series of patients with a particular condition at a certain hospital, "normal" graduate students walking down the hall are asked to donate blood for a study, children with febrile seizures reporting to an emergency room</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Investigator decides who is enrolled in a study</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
49
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>A consecutive sample</li>
<li>A case series of consecutive patients with a condition of interest</li>
<li>Consecutive series means ALL patients with the condition within hospital or clinic, not just the patients the investigators happen to know about</li>
<li>Advantages</li>
<li>Removes investigator from deciding who enters a study</li>
<li>Requires protocol with definitions of condition of interest</li>
<li>Straightforward way to enrol subjects</li>
<li>Disadvantage</li>
<li>Non-random</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Snowball</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>The researcher asks the respondents to give referrals to other possible respondents</li>
<li>Used in studies in populations where it is difficult to get respondents e.g. drug addicts, homeless people, individuals with HIV/AIDS, prostitutes</li>
<li>Such populations are hard to reach and/or hidden because they exhibit some kind of social stigma</li>
</ul>
<ol start="3">
<li>Purposive sampling (Judgemental/Selective)</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>We sample with a purpose in mind</li>
<li>Focus on particular characteristics of a population that are of interest</li>
<li>Types – maximum variation, homeogenous, typical case, extreme(deviant) case, critical case, expert and total population sampling</li>
</ul>
<ol start="4">
<li>Quota sample</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Researcher selects respondents according to a some fixed quota (representative group)</li>
<li>The representative individuals are chosen out of a specific group</li>
</ul>
<ol start="5">
<li>Volunteer sample</li>
<li>Capture-recapture</li>
</ol>
<p>3.0. DATA ANALYSIS</p>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lesson-7-disease-screening-in-populations">Lesson 7: DISEASE SCREENING IN POPULATIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lesson-7-disease-screening-in-populations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>At the end of the lesson the learner will be able to: -</p>
<ol>
<li>Describe types of screening and screening tests</li>
<li>Discuss characteristics of screening tests</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-0-introduction">1.0. INTRODUCTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-0-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Different kinds of testing in medicine</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>"Diagnostic" - specifically looking for a suspected condition which is tested for and confirmed or excluded</li>
<li>"Case-finding" - usually in an investigation of exposed people, to sort the exposed and ill from the exposed and well (E.g., test people who were in contact with a case of tuberculosis, or check blood pressure of patient who is overweight)</li>
<li>"Screening" - usually no specific exposure or indication that the individual has disease. (E.g. routine PSA testing in middle-aged males)</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-0-screening">2.0. SCREENING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-0-screening" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Defined as a relatively quick means of detecting potential diseases before it has become manifest</li>
<li>Screening does not establish a diagnosis but helps to identify individuals who appear to have a given disease and who should, therefore, undergo further testing to determine if the disease is actually present.</li>
<li>Screening for diseases detection is valuable where early identification of a disease leads to more effective treatment and a better prognosis for the individual e.g. screening for breast, colorectal, cervical cancer and screening for hypertension, diabetes and rubella.</li>
<li>Depending on its intent, screening can take place at different stages in the natural history of disease and can represent different levels of prevention.</li>
<li>Screening is a mode of primary or secondary prevention</li>
<li>Screening programs, especially mass screening, can be expensive and time consuming</li>
<li>Require valuable resources</li>
<li>Examples of disease and conditions for screening include neonatal hypothyroidism, hypertension, breast cancer, cervical cancer, syphilis, gonorrhoea, cystic fibrosis, colorectal cancer, diabetes, tuberculosis, diminished visual acuity, hearing impairment, elevated cholesterol, lead poisoning, HIV infection, Down's syndrome, iron deficiency anaemia, neural tube defects and obesity</li>
<li>Examples of diagnostic/screening tests include mammography (preclinical breast cancer), alpha-fetoprotein (baby with neural tube defect), electrocardiography (heart disease), serological testing (e.g. infection) and somatic cell counts (subclinical mastitis in cattle)</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
51
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-0-reasons-for-screening">3.0. REASONS FOR SCREENING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-0-reasons-for-screening" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Identify risk factors for disease</li>
<li>Reduce morbidity and mortality</li>
<li>Improved quality of life</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-0-considerations-for-screening">4.0. CONSIDERATIONS FOR SCREENING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-0-considerations-for-screening" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Nature of the disease - the disease should
a) Be a significant public health problem that can be detected before symptoms develop
b) Be one that the public views as important enough to submit to screening
c) One that is likely to yield enough cases for the screening program to be cost effective
d) Have a relatively high prevalence in pre-symptomatic stage.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Nature of the screening test
a) Should be accurate (valid and precise)
b) Relatively quick and simple to apply
c) Easily interpreted
d) Safe
e) Acceptable to those being screened
f) Relatively inexpensive</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Nature of the follow-up tests
a) Must be available to confirm the suspected diagnosis
b) Should be readily accessible, accurate, generally safe, and acceptable to those with presumed disease from the standpoint of comfort and cost</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Nature of the treatment
a) Must be available, accessible, and acceptable to patients, and it must be more effective than if it or another treatment were initiated at a later stage of the disease
b) Should result in significantly better prognosis when the disease is treated in its early stages before symptoms arise</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="5-0-types-of-screening">5.0. TYPES OF SCREENING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-0-types-of-screening" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Screening for disease detection is often categorized into the following four types:</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Mass screening</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Aimed at large population groups that vary widely in their risk of the disease e.g. screening for visual impairment in elementary schools is another example of mass screening</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Selective screening</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Applied only to groups at high risk for the disease e.g. screening for elevated blood lead levels among inner-city children is an example of selective screening. So is screening for tuberculosis among prison inmates.</li>
<li>Expected to detect more potential cases of a given disease than mass screening because of the difference in risk profiles between the populations being screening</li>
<li>Is sometimes referred to as targeted screening.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="3">
<li>Multiphase screening</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Employs multiple screening tests at the same time</li>
<li>May be used to detect the possibility of more than one disease or condition.</li>
<li>Paramilitary exams, for example, may use multiphase screening to test for possible diabetes, hypertension, and hearing impairment.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="4">
<li>Case finding</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Occurs in a clinical setting when patients visit their physician (or other health provider) for general consultation or unrelated problems, and physician takes the opportunity to request one or more routine screening tests.</li>
<li>Places the responsibility for follow-up on the physician performing or supervising the screening test. Therefore, case finding is more likely to result in follow-up than other types of screening</li>
<li>Many individuals identified as having elevated blood pressure during a mass screening, for example, may not seek the recommended follow-up, but a physician finding elevated blood pressure during a routine examination will ordinary schedule additional tests.</li>
<li>Examples of case finding include screening for cervical cancer using Pap tests, heart abnormalities using an electrocardiogram, weight changes using a calibrated scale, and diabetes using blood tests or urine samples. In addition, optometrists and ophthalmologists routinely screen patients for glaucoma</li>
<li>Case finding has been referred to as opportunistic screening</li>
</ul>
<p>6.0. PRINCIPLES FOR SCREENING PROGRAMS</p>
<ol>
<li>Condition should be an important health problem</li>
<li>There should be a recognizable early or latent stage</li>
<li>There should be an accepted treatment for persons with condition</li>
<li>The screening test is valid, reliable, with acceptable yield</li>
<li>The test should be acceptable to the population to be screened</li>
<li>The cost of screening and case finding should be economically balanced in relation to medical care as a whole</li>
</ol>
