<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="7-0-characteristics-of-a-good-screening-test">7.0. CHARACTERISTICS OF A GOOD SCREENING TEST<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-0-characteristics-of-a-good-screening-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="7-1-validity-of-screening-tests">7.1. Validity of Screening Tests<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-1-validity-of-screening-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Defined as its ability to distinguish between who has a disease and who does not</li>
<li>Has 2 components — <strong>sensitivity</strong> (ability of a test to identify correctly those who have the disease or true positives) and <strong>specificity</strong> (ability of a test to identify correctly those who do not have a disease or true negatives)</li>
<li>The validity of a screening test is measured by its ability to correctly categorize persons who have pre-clinical disease as test-positive and those without pre-clinical disease as test-negative</li>
<li>The precision of a screening test refers to its reliability, that is, its consistency from one application to the next</li>
<li>A positive test implies that the disease is likely to present, and follow-up diagnostic tests are therefore advisable</li>
<li>A negative test implies that the disease is unlikely to be present, and follow-up diagnostic are therefore not indicated.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-380eccec.jpeg" alt="img-20.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sensitivity-of-screening-test">Sensitivity of Screening Test<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sensitivity-of-screening-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The probability of a positive test result given that the individual tested actually has the disease</li>
<li>A test with high sensitivity will have few false negatives</li>
</ul>
<table><thead><tr><th>Mammography</th><th></th><th>Disease</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Cancer</td><td>No cancer</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Positive</td><td>132 (TP)</td><td>985 (FP)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Negative</td><td>47 (FN)</td><td>62,295 (TN)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Total</td><td>179 (TP + FN)</td><td>63,280 (FP + TN)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Sensitivity = TP/(TP + FN)</p>
<p>= 132 / 179 = 73.7%</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specificity-of-screening-test">Specificity of Screening Test<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specificity-of-screening-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The probability of a negative test result given that the individual tested actually does not have the disease</li>
<li>A test that has high specificity will have few false positives</li>
</ul>
<p>EXAMPLE: Mammographic screening in the Health Insurance Plan (HIP) New York</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Mammography</th><th></th><th>Disease</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Cancer</td><td>No cancer</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Positive</td><td>132 (TP)</td><td>985 (FP)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Negative</td><td>47 (FN)</td><td>62,295 (TN)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Total</td><td>179 (TP + FN)</td><td>63,280 (FP + TN)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Specificity = TN/(FP + TN)</p>
<p>= 62,295 / 63,280 = 98.4%</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="relating-specificity-and-sensitivity">Relating Specificity and Sensitivity<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#relating-specificity-and-sensitivity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>True Diagnosis</th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Test Results</td><td>Disease</td><td>No Disease</td><td>Total</td></tr><tr><td>Positive</td><td>a (TP)</td><td>b (FP)</td><td>a + b (TP + FP)</td></tr><tr><td>Negative</td><td>c (FN)</td><td>d (TN)</td><td>c + d (FN + TN)</td></tr><tr><td>Total</td><td>a + c (TP + FN)</td><td>b + d (FP+TN)</td><td>a + b + c + d (TP + FP + FN + TN)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul>
<li>Specificity + false positive rate = 1</li>
<li>(d/(b+d) + b/(b+d) = 1)</li>
<li>If the specificity is increased, the false positive rate is decreased</li>
<li>If the specificity is decreased, the false positive rate is increased</li>
<li>Sensitivity + false negative rate = 1</li>
<li>(a/(a+c) + c/(a+c) = 1)</li>
<li>If the sensitivity is increased, the false negative rate is decreased</li>
<li>If the sensitivity is decreased, the false negative rate is increased</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="predictive-value">Predictive Value<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#predictive-value" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Predictive value of a positive test is the likelihood that a person with a positive test has the disease (the probability that an individual actually has the disease given that he or she tests positive)</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Predictive value of a negative test is the likelihood that a person with a negative test does not have the disease (the probability that an individual is disease-free given that he or she tests negative)</li>
<li>Predictive value depends on the prevalence of disease</li>
</ul>
<p>EXAMPLE: Mammographic screening in the Health Insurance Plan (HIP) New York</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Mammography</th><th></th><th>Disease</th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Cancer</td><td>No cancer</td><td>Total</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Positive</td><td>132 (TP)</td><td>985 (FP)</td><td>1117 (TP + FP)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Negative</td><td>47 (FN)</td><td>62,295 (TN)</td><td>62,342 (FN + TN)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Total</td><td>179 (TP + FN)</td><td>63,280 (FP + TN)</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Positive Predictive Value (PPV) - proportion of test positives that truly have the condition</p>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mtable rowspacing="0.16em" columnalign="left" columnspacing="1em"><mtr><mtd><mstyle scriptlevel="0" displaystyle="false"><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">V</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">T</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi></mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">T</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi></mrow><mo>+</mo><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">F</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mtd></mtr><mtr><mtd><mstyle scriptlevel="0" displaystyle="false"><mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>132</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mn>1117</mn><mo>=</mo><mn>11.8</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">%</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>12</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">%</mi><mtext>&nbsp;(88&nbsp;false&nbsp;positives&nbsp;out&nbsp;of&nbsp;100&nbsp;positive&nbsp;tests)</mtext></mrow></mstyle></mtd></mtr></mtable><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\begin{array}{l}

<p>Negative Predictive Value (NPV) - proportion of test negatives that truly do not have the condition</p>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mtable rowspacing="0.16em" columnalign="left" columnspacing="1em"><mtr><mtd><mstyle scriptlevel="0" displaystyle="false"><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">V</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">T</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi></mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">T</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi></mrow><mo>+</mo><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">F</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mtd></mtr><mtr><mtd><mstyle scriptlevel="0" displaystyle="false"><mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>62</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mn>295</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mn>62</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mn>342</mn><mo>=</mo><mn>99.9</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">%</mi></mrow></mstyle></mtd></mtr></mtable><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\begin{array}{l}

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="7-2-reliability-precision-of-screening-test">7.2. Reliability (Precision) Of Screening Test<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-2-reliability-precision-of-screening-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Reliability is the ability of a test to give consistent results when performed more than once on the same individual under the same conditions</li>
<li>Can be improved through</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Variation in the method due to variability of test chemicals or fluctuation in the item measured (e.g., diurnal variation in body temperature or in relation to meals)</li>
<li>Standardize fluctuating variables</li>
<li>Use standards in laboratory tests, run multiple samples whenever possible</li>
<li>Observer variation (train observers and use more than one observer and have them check each other)</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="7-3-yield-of-screening-test">7.3. Yield of Screening Test<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-3-yield-of-screening-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Is the amount of previously unrecognized disease that is diagnosed and brought to treatment as a result of the screening program</li>
<li>Results from</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol>
<li>Sensitivity - you must detect a sufficient population of disease to be useful</li>
<li>Prevalence of unrecognized disease - screen high risk populations</li>
<li>Frequency of screening - screening on a one time basis does not allow for the natural history of the disease, differences in individual risk, or differences in onset and diseases have lead time</li>
<li>Participation and follow-up - tests unacceptable to those targeted for screening will not be utilized</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="7-4-efficacy-of-screening-test">7.4. Efficacy of Screening Test<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-4-efficacy-of-screening-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Benefits of a treatment, procedure, or service among those who use it compared to those who don't</li>
<li>Measured by the degree to which it benefits those who are screened compare to those who are not</li>
<li>Most evaluations of the efficacy of screening are not based on randomized controlled trials, however, often because of costs, feasibility, or ethical limitations</li>
<li>More often than not, evaluations of screening utilize observational methods or other nonrandomized designs that are more subject to potential sources of bias, such as volunteer bias, lead time bias, and length bias</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-0-bias-in-screening">8.0. BIAS IN SCREENING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-0-bias-in-screening" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Referral Bias, compliance (volunteer bias)</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Volunteers or compliers are better educated and more health conscious – thus they have inherently better prognosis</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Length Bias</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Screening selectively identifies those with a long preclinical and clinical phase (i.e., those who would have a better prognosis regardless of the screening program)</li>
</ul>
<ol start="3">
<li>Lead Time Bias</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>The apparently better survival that is observed for those screened is not because these patients are actually living longer, but instead because diagnosis is being made at an earlier point in the natural history of the disease</li>
</ul>
<ol start="4">
<li>Over-diagnosis Bias (a misclassification bias)</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Enthusiasm for a new screening program may result in a higher rate of false positives and give false impression of increased rates of diagnosis and detection</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="9-0-screening-tests">9.0. SCREENING TESTS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#9-0-screening-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Identify at least thirty (30) screening tests and state their use in clinical and epidemiological practice in Kenya,</p>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
5
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lesson-8-disease-surveillance">Lesson 8: DISEASE SURVEILLANCE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lesson-8-disease-surveillance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>At the end of the lesson the learner will be able to: -</p>
<ol>
<li>Explain the purpose of surveillance</li>
<li>Describe the various epidemiological surveillance systems</li>
<li>Discuss the uses of epidemiological surveillance data</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="1-0-introduction">1.0. INTRODUCTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-0-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Disease surveillance is an information-based activity involving the collection, analysis and interpretation of large volumes of data originating from a variety of sources.</li>
<li>Surveillance is</li>
<li>i) The ongoing, systematic collection of data related to health events; their verification, analysis, interpretation, and the dissemination of information to those who need to know in order to reduce morbidity and mortality &amp; to improve health (WHO)</li>
<li>ii) Ongoing systematic collection, analysis, and interpretation of health data essential to the planning, implementation, &amp; evaluation of public health practice, closely integrated with the timely dissemination of the data to those who need to know (CDC)</li>
<li>Epidemiological information can be used to develop prevention strategies according to person (groups at risk), place and time (peaks at a particular season)</li>
<li>In emergencies, epidemiology has three elements:</li>
</ul>
<p>i) Descriptive epidemiology</p>
<ul>
<li>Determines the distribution of a disease among displaced populations</li>
<li>Describes the health problem, its frequency, who, where, and when</li>
<li>Examples</li>
<li>a) Monitoring the health status of a population to detect cholera cases, such as, by age, sex, location, water source and duration of stay in a dispersed population or camps</li>
<li>b) Conducting a nutritional survey to determine the prevalence of acute malnutrition among children under five</li>
</ul>
<p>ii) Analytical epidemiology</p>
<ul>
<li>Compares those who are ill with those who are not in order to identify the risk of disease or protective factors (determinant of a disease)</li>
<li>Examines how the event (illness, death, malnutrition, injury) is caused (e.g. environmental and behavioural factors) and why it is continuing</li>
<li>Standard mathematical and statistical procedures are used</li>
<li>Example: Investigating an outbreak of an unknown disease in a displaced population settlement</li>
</ul>
<p>iii) Evaluation epidemiology</p>
<ul>
<li>Examines the relevance, effectiveness and impact of different programme activities in relation to the health of the affected populations</li>
<li>Example - evaluating a malaria control programme for displaced populations</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>2.0. PURPOSE</p>
<ol>
<li>Morbidity and mortality reporting</li>
<li>Monitoring disease trends</li>
<li>Describing natural history of diseases</li>
<li>Documenting of distribution and spread of disease.</li>
<li>Establish long term trends in disease occurrence.</li>
<li>Detect epidemics, outbreaks or new syndromes</li>
<li>Identify high risk groups or areas.</li>
<li>Facilitating planning of control and disease prevention.</li>
<li>Evaluation of intervention measures</li>
<li>Resource allocation and planning</li>
<li>Setting research priorities</li>
<li>Monitor implementation and effectiveness of programmes</li>
</ol>
<p>3.0. SOURCES OF INFORMATION</p>
<ol>
<li>Health facilities</li>
<li>Death and birth registers</li>
<li>Laboratories</li>
<li>The community self</li>
<li>Special search e.g. Chickenpox</li>
<li>Investigation of Outbreaks</li>
<li>Surveys</li>
</ol>
<p>4.0. TYPE OF INFORMATION</p>
<p>The Person</p>
<ul>
<li>When available, demographic characteristics such as gender, age, race/ethnicity, occupation, education level, socio-economic status, sexual orientation, immunization status can reveal disease trends</li>
<li>Example: looking at Streptococcus pneumoniae, a common cause of community-acquired pneumonia and bacterial meningitis, examining distribution of cases by race provides important information about burden of disease in different populations</li>
</ul>
<p>Place</p>
<ul>
<li>Best to characterize cases by place of exposure rather than by place at which cases reported as the two may differ and place of exposure is more relevant to epidemiology of a disease;</li>
<li>Example: travellers on a cruise ship exposed to a disease just prior to disembarking but become symptomatic and are diagnosed after return to various home locations; Example: person exposed to disease in small rural town but referred to tertiary care centre 100 miles away where disease is diagnosed and reported</li>
<li>Data by geographic location can be presented in a table</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Inferential analysis can also be done using multilevel modelling, other statistical methods</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="time">Time<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#time" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Compare number of cases reported in time period of interest (weeks, months, years) to number of cases reported during similar historical period</li>
<li>Usually a delay between disease onset and date when disease is reported, so preferable to use date of onset, if available, rather than date of report especially helpful for examining data not likely to have much short term variation example: there is limited variation in number of AIDS cases reported each month</li>
<li>Provide valuable qualitative information; disease outbreaks often obvious from visual inspection of data, may not require a quantitative analysis</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-0-process">5.0. PROCESS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-0-process" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Detect priority diseases</li>
<li>Plan for surveillance</li>
<li>Collect and compile information (tally daily/frequencies tables, prepare disease maps, investigate all cases e.g. cholera, total all cases daily, monthly)</li>
<li>Analyse and interpret data</li>
<li>Supervise and give feedback</li>
<li>Monitoring and Evaluation</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-0-surveillance-systems">6.0. SURVEILLANCE SYSTEMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-0-surveillance-systems" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="6-1-criteria-of-evaluating-surveillance-systems">6.1. Criteria of Evaluating Surveillance Systems<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-1-criteria-of-evaluating-surveillance-systems" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Generally evaluated according to the following attributes such as simplicity, flexibility, acceptability, sensitivity, positive predictive value, representativeness (completeness, public/private), timeliness and adequately resourced (cost of training, travel, supplies, equipment and services)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="6-2-surveillance-systems">6.2. Surveillance Systems<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-2-surveillance-systems" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Comprises of five components namely: - defining target events/diseases; defining structure, core functions, support functions and measuring surveillance attributes</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>First Component: Defining Target Events/Diseases</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Prioritized events selected for surveillance</li>
<li>Based on disease burden, case fatality, potential to control, potential to cause outbreaks, potential to cross borders</li>
<li>Defining the objective of the control</li>
<li>Control - to reduce mortality and morbidity</li>
<li>Elimination - prevent outbreaks &amp; limit indigenous transmission of infectious agent</li>
<li>Eradication - to subtract the infectious agent from the global earth.</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Examples</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Immediate Notification</th><th>Weekly or monthly notification</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Polio, measles, cholera and food poisoning</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251226-7810f15b.jpeg" alt="img-21.jpeg">
Components of a Surveillance System</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-second-component-defining-the-structure">2) Second Component - Defining the Structure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-second-component-defining-the-structure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>a. Legislation (laws and regulation)
b. Surveillance strategy</p>
<ul>
<li>Indicator-based - specific indicators reaches specified thresholds, number of cases, weekly incidence and relative increase</li>
<li>Event-based - unofficial reports, rumours, health and non-health sources</li>
</ul>
<p>c. Alerts - verification of outbreak or false alert
d. Implementers and stakeholders
e. Data and information required
f. Case definition - based on common understanding by professionals for targeted diseases/events and clinical, epidemiological, laboratory and other para-clinical findings/confirmed/probable/suspected cases</p>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Third Component – Core Functions</li>
</ol>
<table><thead><tr><th>Core Functions</th><th>Details</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Case detection</td><td>By clinicians -data sources</td></tr><tr><td>Case registration</td><td>By data sources, in medical records or registers</td></tr><tr><td>Case confirmation</td><td>Laboratory tests to confirm cases or discard them</td></tr><tr><td>Case notification</td><td>By physicians to surveillance officers, on regular or irregular basis; immediately, weekly or monthly; written on paper or electronically</td></tr><tr><td>Case investigation</td><td>By surveillance officers to collect additional data and to collect samples</td></tr><tr><td>Data analysis</td><td>Transform data into information &amp; displayed it by time, place &amp; person.</td></tr><tr><td>Information Communication (feedback)</td><td>Feed-back to data sources; forward to higher level and dissemination to professionals and the public</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ol start="4">
<li>Fourth Component – Support Functions</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Standards and guidelines, training sessions, supervision, coordination, laboratory support, monitoring and evaluation, information and Communication technology</li>
</ul>
<ol start="5">
<li>Fifth Component – Attributes of the Surveillance</li>
</ol>
<table><thead><tr><th>Attribute</th><th>Examples</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Quantitative attributes</td><td>Completeness; Timeliness, Positive predictive value; Sensitivity</td></tr><tr><td>Qualitative attributes</td><td>Usefulness; Simplicity, Acceptability; Flexibility; Representativeness</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-3-key-elements-of-a-surveillance-system">6.3. Key Elements of A surveillance System<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-3-key-elements-of-a-surveillance-system" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Detection and notification of health events</li>
<li>Investigation and confirmation</li>
<li>Data collection</li>
<li>Analysis and interpretation of data</li>
<li>Feedback and dissemination of results</li>
<li>Response – action for prevention and control</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-4-types-of-surveillance">6.4. Types of Surveillance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-4-types-of-surveillance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-passive-surveillance">1) Passive surveillance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-passive-surveillance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Involves collection of data from existing unsolicited reports of the diseases(s)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Health authorities do not stimulate reporting by reminding health care workers to report disease nor providing feedback to individual health workers.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Most common type of surveillance in humanitarian emergencies &amp; communicable diseases</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Health workers are trained on how to complete the surveillance forms (collected periodically)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Data is often incomplete because there are few incentives for health workers to report</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Sources include reports such as notification forms filled by nurses of health department, solicited reports.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is good for conditions that have clear symptomatology e.g. measles.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>It is less costly than active surveillance</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-active-surveillance">2) Active surveillance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-active-surveillance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Involves active finding cases of the disease; for example, calling medical facilities (e.g., laboratories or emergency departments) or sending field teams to hospitals to extract information from hospital records</li>
<li>Reporting frequency by individual health workers is monitored; health workers who consistently fail to report or complete the forms incorrectly are provided specific feedback to improve their performance. There may also be incentives provided for complete reporting.</li>
<li>Requires substantially more time and resources and is therefore less commonly used in emergencies</li>
<li>Often more complete than passive surveillance</li>
<li>Used if an outbreak has begun or is suspected to keep close track of the number of cases</li>
<li>Community health workers may be asked to do active case finding in the community in order to detect those patients who may not come to health facilities for treatment.</li>
<li>Is used for disease which can be easily missed e.g. malaria with few asymptomatic infections. It remind health workers to be on the lookout for these conditions.</li>
<li>It need laboratory confirmation because of its nonspecific clinical syndrome</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-sentinel-surveillance">3) Sentinel surveillance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-sentinel-surveillance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A sentinel surveillance system selects, either randomly or intentionally, a small group of health workers from whom to gather data</li>
<li>The health workers then receive greater attention from health authorities than would be possible with universal surveillance</li>
<li>Requires more time and resources, produce more detailed data on cases of illness because the health care workers have agreed to participate and may receive incentives</li>
<li>Best type of surveillance if more intensive investigation of each case is necessary to collect the necessary data</li>
<li>Use data from a few selected sites rather than data from all sites e.g. clinic</li>
<li>Since it is from selected sites it provide accurate and more complete data on the sentinel population they are more quick available more reliable and less costly.</li>
<li>Most of them are passive surveillance</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-syndromic-surveillance">4) Syndromic surveillance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-syndromic-surveillance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Is the collection and analysis of non-specific data from multiple data sources to detect a possible change or trend in the health of a population</li>
</ul>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015
This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Involves collection and analysis of syndrome-related data, but has expanded to include almost any non-specific data from multiple sources that may indicate a potential biologic event has occurred</li>
<li>Data sources may include: data from hospital emergency departments or other emergency encounters, physician office visits, over-the-counter pharmaceutical sales, and school absenteeism records</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-outbreak-surveillance">5) Outbreak Surveillance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-outbreak-surveillance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Undertaken to establish the source and cause of the outbreak with the aim of quick control with targeted interventions</li>
<li>Also provide information on vaccine efficacy, age specific attack rate and case fatality</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-5-epidemiological-indicators">6.5. Epidemiological indicators<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-5-epidemiological-indicators" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Indicators are measures that reflect the state of a population in terms of health and socioeconomic status</li>
<li>Indicators may be quantitative or qualitative in nature</li>
<li>Quantitative indicators are easily calculated from numeric information such as the total number of people, the number of people according to age and sex etc. Examples of quantitative indicators include incidence, prevalence, morbidity and mortality rate</li>
<li>Qualitative indicators that measure people's attitudes and knowledge are more difficult to measure. These indicators might be critical in explaining unexpected values of quantitative indicators. Social processes influencing health outcomes might also be elucidated by using qualitative indicators.</li>
<li>Examples of qualitative indicators include</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Awareness of the value of immunization—low awareness may explain the high incidence of measles in a population living within five kilometres from a health facility</li>
<li>Adherence to preventive interventions against HIV/AIDS—poor compliance from youths in preventive interventions (e.g.; A lack of understanding about the "Abstinence, Be Faithful, Use a Condom" (ABC) programme)) might explain the increasing prevalence of HIV/AIDS in a population</li>
<li>Equity in distribution of resources—inequitable distribution of food might explain the increased mortality detected in a subgroup of a population</li>
<li>Barriers to seeking treatment for malaria—barriers to seeking treatment such as unaffordable health services, might explain an increase in malaria-specific mortality.</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Qualitative indicators commonly used for assessing programme outcomes:</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Access - the proportion of the target population that can use the service or facility</li>
<li>Coverage - the proportion of the target population that has received service</li>
<li>Quality of services - the actual services received compared with the standards and guidelines</li>
<li>Availability - amount of services compared with total target population. This should be based on minimum standard requirements</li>
</ol>
<p>Carey Francis Okinda © 2015</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>7.0. DATA PRESENTATION</p>
<ol>
<li>Line graphs for displaying data by time</li>
<li>Maps for presenting data in geographic context</li>
<li>Graphical displays such as histograms, frequency polygons, box plots, scatter diagrams, bar charts, pie charts, or stem-and-leaf displays</li>
<li>Spot maps</li>
<li>Single/multivariable tables</li>
</ol>
<p>8.0. USES OF SURVEILLANCE DATA</p>
<ol>
<li>Priority setting and planning</li>
<li>Resources mobilization and allocation</li>
<li>Prediction and early detection of epidemics</li>
<li>Early and adequate detection and response</li>
<li>Monitoring and evaluation of intervention programmes</li>
<li>Identify high risk groups</li>
<li>Increase knowledge of vectors, dynamics of transmission</li>
<li>Establish long-term trends and pattern in disease occurrence</li>
<li>Serve a background which allows for detection on unusual pattern of a disease</li>
<li>Trigger disease control effort</li>
</ol>
<p>9.0. CHALLENGES</p>
<ol>
<li>Failure to report on time</li>
<li>Incomplete and late reporting</li>
<li>Inadequate data analysis</li>
<li>Failure to use available information to check trends</li>
<li>Poor feedback to health workers and communities</li>
<li>Duplication of efforts</li>
<li>Underutilization of surveillance information in decision making</li>
</ol>
<p>10.0. PROBLEMS</p>
<ol>
<li>Poor knowledge of conditions that are notifiable</li>
<li>Notification forms are too complicated and too many details are requested</li>
<li>Lack of feedback on reported cases</li>
<li>Lack of adequate training in surveillance</li>
<li>Understaffing (workers too busy to spend a lot time filling in the notification forms)</li>
<li>Incomplete filling of notification forms</li>
<li>Administrative delays of reporting hamper also timely and effective disease control</li>
<li>Lack of regular evaluation and validation of surveillance systems and processes</li>
<li>Active surveillance is not implemented widely because of its higher cost</li>
<li>Biased selection of sentinel sites (results are unrepresentative of the total population)</li>
</ol>
